   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Colonization of the Moon
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
     <span class="mw-redirectedfrom">
      (Redirected from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Moonbase&amp;redirect=no" title="Moonbase">
       Moonbase
      </a>
      )
     </span>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       "Lunar outpost" redirects here. For NASA's former plan to construct an outpost between 2019 and 2024, see
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_outpost_(NASA)" title="Lunar outpost (NASA)">
        Lunar outpost (NASA)
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       "Moonbase" redirects here. For other uses, see
       <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Moonbase_(disambiguation)" title="Moonbase (disambiguation)">
        Moonbase (disambiguation)
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:327px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moon_colony_with_rover.jpeg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3449" data-file-width="5424" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Moon_colony_with_rover.jpeg/325px-Moon_colony_with_rover.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Moon_colony_with_rover.jpeg/488px-Moon_colony_with_rover.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Moon_colony_with_rover.jpeg/650px-Moon_colony_with_rover.jpeg 2x" width="325"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moon_colony_with_rover.jpeg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         1986 artist concept
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        colonization of the Moon
       </b>
       is a proposed establishment of permanent human communities or robotic industries
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       on the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Discovery of
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_water" title="Lunar water">
        lunar water
       </a>
       at the
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_south_pole" title="Lunar south pole">
        lunar poles
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Chandrayaan-1" title="Chandrayaan-1">
        Chandrayaan-1
       </a>
       has renewed interest in the Moon. Polar colonies could also avoid the problem of long lunar nights – about 354 hours,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a little more than two weeks – and take advantage of the Sun continuously, at least during the local summer (there is no data for the winter yet).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Permanent human habitation on a planetary body other than the Earth is one of science fiction's most prevalent themes. As technology has advanced, and concerns about the
       <a href="/wiki/Spaceship_Earth" title="Spaceship Earth">
        future of humanity on Earth
       </a>
       have increased, the argument that
       <a href="/wiki/Space_colonization" title="Space colonization">
        space colonization
       </a>
       as an achievable and worthwhile goal has gained momentum.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spaceref_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-spaceref-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-race_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-race-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Because of its proximity to Earth, the Moon has been seen as the most obvious natural expansion after Earth. There are also various projects planned for the near future by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_tourism_startup_companies" title="Space tourism startup companies">
        space tourism startup companies
       </a>
       for
       <a href="/wiki/Tourism_on_the_Moon" title="Tourism on the Moon">
        tourism on the Moon
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

           External links
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
       </ul>
      </div>
      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Proposals">
        Proposals
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Proposals">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="toccolours nomobile" style="text-align:left;font-size:0.9em;padding: 0.2em; float:right; margin-left:1em">
       <tr>

           Space colonization
          </a>
         </b>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>

         <ul>

               </a>

       <tr>
        <td style="float:right">
         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar">
          <span style="word-spacing:0">
           This box:
          </span>
          <ul>
           <li class="nv-view">
            <a href="/wiki/Template:Space_colonization" title="Template:Space colonization">
             <span title="View this template">
              view
             </span>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-talk">
            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Space_colonization" title="Template talk:Space colonization">
             <span title="Discuss this template">
              talk
             </span>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-edit">
            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Space_colonization&amp;action=edit">
             <span title="Edit this template">
              edit
             </span>
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Entering_a_Lunar_Outpost.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1687" data-file-width="3000" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Entering_a_Lunar_Outpost.jpg/220px-Entering_a_Lunar_Outpost.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Entering_a_Lunar_Outpost.jpg/330px-Entering_a_Lunar_Outpost.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Entering_a_Lunar_Outpost.jpg/440px-Entering_a_Lunar_Outpost.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Entering_a_Lunar_Outpost.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Concept art from NASA showing astronauts entering a lunar outpost
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The notion of a lunar colony originated before the
       <a href="/wiki/Space_Age" title="Space Age">
        Space Age
       </a>
       . In 1638 Bishop
       <a href="/wiki/John_Wilkins" title="John Wilkins">
        John Wilkins
       </a>
       wrote
       <i>
        A Discourse Concerning a New World and Another Planet,
       </i>
       in which he predicted a human colony on the Moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Konstantin_Eduardovich_Tsiolkovskii" title="Konstantin Eduardovich Tsiolkovskii">
        Konstantin Tsiolkovsky
       </a>
       (1857–1935), among others, also suggested such a step.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       From the 1950s onwards, a number of concepts and designs have been suggested by scientists, engineers and others.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1954, science-fiction writer
       <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">
        Arthur C. Clarke
       </a>
       proposed a lunar base of inflatable modules covered in lunar dust for insulation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-base_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-base-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A spaceship, assembled in
       <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">
        low Earth orbit
       </a>
       , would launch to the Moon, and astronauts would set up the
       <a href="/wiki/Igloo" title="Igloo">
        igloo
       </a>
       -like modules and an inflatable
       <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">
        radio
       </a>
       mast. Subsequent steps would include the establishment of a larger, permanent dome; an
       <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">
        algae
       </a>
       -based
       <a href="/wiki/Air_purifier" title="Air purifier">
        air purifier
       </a>
       ; a
       <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">
        nuclear reactor
       </a>
       for the provision of power; and
       <a href="/wiki/Mass_driver" title="Mass driver">
        electromagnetic cannons
       </a>
       to launch
       <a href="/wiki/Cargo" title="Cargo">
        cargo
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">
        fuel
       </a>
       to interplanetary vessels in space.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1959,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=John_S._Rinehart&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="John S. Rinehart (page does not exist)">
        John S. Rinehart
       </a>
       suggested that the safest design would be a structure that could "[float] in a stationary ocean of dust", since there were, at the time this concept was outlined, theories that there could be mile-deep dust oceans on the Moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The proposed design consisted of a half-cylinder with half-domes at both ends, with a
       <a href="/wiki/Micrometeoroid" title="Micrometeoroid">
        micrometeoroid
       </a>
       shield placed above the base.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Project_Horizon">
        Project Horizon
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Project Horizon">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Project_Horizon" title="Project Horizon">
        Project Horizon
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Project Horizon was a 1959 study regarding the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">
        United States Army
       </a>
       's plan to establish a fort on the Moon by 1967.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Heinz-Hermann_Koelle" title="Heinz-Hermann Koelle">
        Heinz-Hermann Koelle
       </a>
       , a German rocket engineer of the
       <a href="/wiki/Army_Ballistic_Missile_Agency" title="Army Ballistic Missile Agency">
        Army Ballistic Missile Agency
       </a>
       (ABMA) led the Project Horizon study. It was proposed that the first landing would be carried out by two "soldier-astronauts" in 1965 and that more construction workers would soon follow. It was posited that through numerous launches (61
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_I" title="Saturn I">
        Saturn I
       </a>
       and 88
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_II" title="Saturn II">
        Saturn II
       </a>
       ), 245 tons of cargo could be transported to the outpost by 1966.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lunex_Project">
        Lunex Project
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Lunex Project">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Lunex_Project" title="Lunex Project">
        Lunex Project
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Lunex Project was a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Air_Force" title="US Air Force">
        US Air Force
       </a>
       plan for a manned lunar landing prior to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_Program" title="Apollo Program">
        Apollo Program
       </a>
       in 1961. It envisaged a 21-airman underground Air Force base on the Moon by 1968 at a total cost of $7.5 billion.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sub-surface_base">
        Sub-surface base
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Sub-surface base">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 1962,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=John_DeNike&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="John DeNike (page does not exist)">
        John DeNike
       </a>
       and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Stanley_Zahn&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Stanley Zahn (page does not exist)">
        Stanley Zahn
       </a>
       published their idea of a sub-surface base located at the
       <a href="/wiki/Mare_Tranquillitatis" title="Mare Tranquillitatis">
        Sea of Tranquility
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-base_9-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-base-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This base would house a crew of 21, in modules placed four meters below the surface, which was believed to provide
       <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">
        radiation
       </a>
       shielding on par with Earth's atmosphere. DeNike and Zahn favored
       <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">
        nuclear reactors
       </a>
       for energy production, because they were more efficient than
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_module" title="Photovoltaic module">
        solar panels
       </a>
       , and would also overcome the problems with the long lunar nights. For the life support system, an algae-based
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gas_exchanger&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gas exchanger (page does not exist)">
        gas exchanger
       </a>
       was proposed.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_proposals">
        Recent proposals
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Recent proposals">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 2007 Jim Burke of the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Space_University" title="International Space University">
        International Space University
       </a>
       in France said people should plan to preserve humanity's culture in the event of a civilization-stopping asteroid impact with Earth. A Lunar
       <a href="/wiki/Noah%27s_Ark" title="Noah's Ark">
        Noah's Ark
       </a>
       was proposed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Subsequent planning may be taken up by the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=International_Lunar_Exploration_Working_Group&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="International Lunar Exploration Working Group (page does not exist)">
        International Lunar Exploration Working Group
       </a>
       (ILEWG).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In November 2017 the International Space University hosted a conference that led to the formation of the Moon Village Association.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2016 Johann-Dietrich Wörner, the new Chief of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESA" title="ESA">
        ESA
       </a>
       , proposed the International Moon Village that incorporates 3D printing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Moon_exploration">
        Moon exploration
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Moon exploration">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration_through_2017">
        Exploration through 2017
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Exploration through 2017">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_the_Moon" title="Exploration of the Moon">
        Exploration of the Moon
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_missions_to_the_Moon" title="List of missions to the Moon">
        List of missions to the Moon
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Exploration of the lunar surface by spacecraft began in 1959 with the
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        Soviet Union
       </a>
       's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luna_program" title="Luna program">
        Luna program
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Luna_1" title="Luna 1">
        Luna 1
       </a>
       missed the Moon, but
       <a href="/wiki/Luna_2" title="Luna 2">
        Luna 2
       </a>
       made a hard landing (impact) into its surface, and became the first artificial object on an extraterrestrial body. The same year, the
       <a href="/wiki/Luna_3" title="Luna 3">
        Luna 3
       </a>
       mission radioed photographs to Earth of the Moon's hitherto unseen
       <a href="/wiki/Far_side_of_the_Moon" title="Far side of the Moon">
        far side
       </a>
       , marking the beginning of a decade-long series of unmanned Lunar explorations.
      </p>
      <p>
       Responding to the Soviet program of space exploration, US President
       <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">
        John F. Kennedy
       </a>
       in 1961 told the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Congress" title="U.S. Congress">
        U.S. Congress
       </a>
       on May 25: "I believe that this nation should commit itself to achieving the goal, before this decade is out, of landing a man on the Moon and returning him safely to the Earth." The same year the Soviet leadership made some of its first public pronouncements about landing a man on the Moon and establishing a Lunar base.
      </p>
      <p>
       Manned exploration of the lunar surface began in 1968 when the
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">
        Apollo 8
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_spacecraft" title="Apollo spacecraft">
        spacecraft
       </a>
       orbited the Moon with three astronauts on board. This was mankind's first direct view of the far side. The following year, the
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">
        Apollo 11
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_module" title="Lunar module">
        Lunar module
       </a>
       landed two astronauts on the Moon, proving the ability of humans to travel to the Moon, perform scientific research work there, and bring back sample materials.
      </p>
      <p>
       Additional missions to the Moon continued this exploration phase. In 1969 the
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_12" title="Apollo 12">
        Apollo 12
       </a>
       mission landed next to the
       <a href="/wiki/Surveyor_3" title="Surveyor 3">
        Surveyor 3
       </a>
       spacecraft, demonstrating precision landing capability. The use of a manned vehicle on the Moon's surface was demonstrated in 1971 with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Rover" title="Lunar Rover">
        Lunar Rover
       </a>
       during
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_15" title="Apollo 15">
        Apollo 15
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_16" title="Apollo 16">
        Apollo 16
       </a>
       made the first landing within the rugged
       <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_the_Moon#Lunar_landscape" title="Geology of the Moon">
        Lunar highlands
       </a>
       . However, interest in further exploration of the Moon was beginning to wane among the American public. In 1972
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">
        Apollo 17
       </a>
       was the final Apollo Lunar mission, and further planned missions were scrapped at the directive of President
       <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">
        Nixon
       </a>
       . Instead, focus was turned to the
       <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">
        Space Shuttle
       </a>
       and manned missions in near Earth orbit.
      </p>
      <p>
       In addition to its scientific returns, the Apollo program also provided valuable lessons about living and working in the lunar environment.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_manned_lunar_programs" title="Soviet manned lunar programs">
        Soviet manned lunar programs
       </a>
       failed to send a manned mission to the Moon. However, in 1966
       <a href="/wiki/Luna_9" title="Luna 9">
        Luna 9
       </a>
       was the first probe to achieve a soft landing and return close-up shots of the Lunar surface.
       <a href="/wiki/Luna_16" title="Luna 16">
        Luna 16
       </a>
       in 1970 returned the first Soviet Lunar soil samples, while in 1970 and 1973 during the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunokhod_program" title="Lunokhod program">
        Lunokhod program
       </a>
       two robotic rovers landed on the Moon.
       <a href="/wiki/Lunokhod_1" title="Lunokhod 1">
        Lunokhod 1
       </a>
       explored the Lunar surface for 322 days, and
       <a href="/wiki/Lunokhod_2" title="Lunokhod 2">
        Lunokhod 2
       </a>
       operated on the Moon about four months only but covered a third more distance. 1974 saw the end of the Soviet Moonshot, two years after the last American manned landing. Besides the manned landings, an abandoned Soviet moon program included building the moonbase "
       <a href="/wiki/Zvezda_(moonbase)" title="Zvezda (moonbase)">
        Zvezda
       </a>
       ", which was the first detailed project with developed mockups of expedition vehicles
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and surface modules.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the decades following, interest in exploring the Moon faded considerably, and only a few dedicated enthusiasts supported a return. However, evidence of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_ice" title="Lunar ice">
        Lunar ice
       </a>
       at the poles gathered by NASA's
       <a href="/wiki/Clementine_(spacecraft)" title="Clementine (spacecraft)">
        Clementine
       </a>
       (1994) and
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Prospector" title="Lunar Prospector">
        Lunar Prospector
       </a>
       (1998) missions rekindled some discussion,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nozette1996_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nozette1996-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as did the potential growth of a
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_space_program" title="Chinese space program">
        Chinese space program
       </a>
       that contemplated its own mission to the Moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Subsequent research suggested that there was far less ice present (if any) than had originally been thought, but that there may still be some usable deposits of hydrogen in other forms.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, in September 2009, the
       <a href="/wiki/Chandrayaan-1" title="Chandrayaan-1">
        Chandrayaan
       </a>
       probe of India, carrying an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISRO" title="ISRO">
        ISRO
       </a>
       instrument, discovered that the Lunar
       <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">
        regolith
       </a>
       contains 0.1% water by weight, overturning theories that had stood for 40 years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2004,
       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
        U.S.
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/President" title="President">
        President
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">
        George W. Bush
       </a>
       called for a
       <a href="/wiki/Vision_for_Space_Exploration" title="Vision for Space Exploration">
        plan
       </a>
       to return manned missions to the Moon by 2020 (since cancelled – see
       <a href="/wiki/Constellation_program" title="Constellation program">
        Constellation program
       </a>
       ). On June 18, 2009 NASA’s
       <a href="/wiki/LCROSS" title="LCROSS">
        LCROSS
       </a>
       /
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter">
        LRO
       </a>
       mission to the moon was launched. The LCROSS mission was designed to acquire research information to assist with future lunar exploratory missions, and was scheduled to conclude with a controlled collision of the craft on the lunar surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       LCROSS's mission concluded as scheduled with its controlled impact on October 9, 2009.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2010, due to reduced congressional NASA appropriations, President
       <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">
        Barack Obama
       </a>
       halted the Bush administration's earlier lunar exploration initiative, and directed a generic focus on manned missions to asteroids and Mars, as well as extending support for the International Space Station.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Planned_manned_lunar_missions_2021_-_2036">
        Planned manned lunar missions 2021 - 2036
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Planned manned lunar missions 2021 - 2036">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       As of 2016, Russia is planning to begin building a human colony on the moon by 2030. Initially, the moon base will be manned by no more than 4 people, with their number later rising to maximum of 12 people.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Japan also has plans to land a man on the moon by 2030,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       while the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">
        People's Republic of China
       </a>
       is currently planning to land a human on the Moon by 2036 (see
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Lunar_Exploration_Program" title="Chinese Lunar Exploration Program">
        Chinese Lunar Exploration Program
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The United States currently (2017) has plans to send a manned space mission to orbit (but not to land on) the moon in 2021.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While the US Trump administration has called for a return of manned missions to the lunar surface, it has currently (2017) not authorized any funding for any such lunar missions in the next 20 years, and instead the current administration has actually reduced the NASA space exploration budget from earlier levels.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Water_discovered_on_Moon">
        Water discovered on Moon
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Water discovered on Moon">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_water" title="Lunar water">
        Lunar water
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:300px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/LRO_Peers_into_Permanent_Shadows.ogv/854px--LRO_Peers_into_Permanent_Shadows.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="39.8" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="LRO_Peers_into_Permanent_Shadows.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c8/LRO_Peers_into_Permanent_Shadows.ogv/LRO_Peers_into_Permanent_Shadows.ogv.480p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="SD WebM (480P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 480P" data-transcodekey="480p.webm" data-width="854" data-height="480" data-bandwidth="1002888" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c8/LRO_Peers_into_Permanent_Shadows.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="HD WebM (720P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 720P" data-transcodekey="720p.webm" data-width="1280" data-height="720" data-bandwidth="1813504" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c8/LRO_Peers_into_Permanent_Shadows.ogv/LRO_Peers_into_Permanent_Shadows.ogv.160p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth WebM (160P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.webm" data-width="284" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="127704" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c8/LRO_Peers_into_Permanent_Shadows.ogv/LRO_Peers_into_Permanent_Shadows.ogv.240p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="252392" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c8/LRO_Peers_into_Permanent_Shadows.ogv/LRO_Peers_into_Permanent_Shadows.ogv.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="503568" data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'>
         <img alt="File:LRO Peers into Permanent Shadows.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/LRO_Peers_into_Permanent_Shadows.ogv/300px--LRO_Peers_into_Permanent_Shadows.ogv.jpg" style="width:300px;height:169px"/>
         <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c8/LRO_Peers_into_Permanent_Shadows.ogv" target="new" title="Play media">
          <span class="play-btn-large">
           <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
            Play media
           </span>
          </span>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LRO_Peers_into_Permanent_Shadows.ogv" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Beginning with a full-frame Moon in this video, the camera flies to the lunar south pole and shows areas of permanent shadow. Realistic shadows evolve through several months.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On 24 September 2009
       <a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">
        <i>
         Science
        </i>
       </a>
       magazine reported that the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon_Mineralogy_Mapper" title="Moon Mineralogy Mapper">
        Moon Mineralogy Mapper
       </a>
       (M
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       ) on the
       <a href="/wiki/Indian_Space_Research_Organisation" title="Indian Space Research Organisation">
        Indian Space Research Organisation
       </a>
       's (ISRO)
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Chandrayaan-1" title="Chandrayaan-1">
         Chandrayaan-1
        </a>
       </i>
       had detected water on the Moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       M
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       detected absorption features near 2.8–3.0 μm (0.00011–0.00012 in) on the surface of the Moon. For silicate bodies, such features are typically attributed to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">
        hydroxyl
       </a>
       - and/or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_(properties)" title="Water (properties)">
        water
       </a>
       -bearing materials. On the Moon, the feature is seen as a widely distributed absorption that appears strongest at cooler high latitudes and at several fresh
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feldspathic" title="Feldspathic">
        feldspathic
       </a>
       craters. The general lack of correlation of this feature in sunlit M
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       data with neutron spectrometer H abundance data suggests that the formation and retention of OH and H
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       O is an ongoing surficial process. OH/H
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       O production processes may feed polar cold traps and make the lunar regolith a candidate source of volatiles for human exploration.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Moon Mineralogy Mapper (M
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       ), an imaging spectrometer, was one of the 11 instruments on board Chandrayaan-1, whose mission came to a premature end on 29 August 2009.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isro_pressrel_090829_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-isro_pressrel_090829-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       M
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       was aimed at providing the first mineral map of the entire lunar surface.
      </p>
      <p>
       Lunar scientists had discussed the possibility of water repositories for decades. They are now increasingly "confident that the decades-long debate is over" a report says. "The Moon, in fact, has water in all sorts of places; not just locked up in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minerals" title="Minerals">
        minerals
       </a>
       , but scattered throughout the broken-up
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_surface" title="Planetary surface">
        surface
       </a>
       , and, potentially, in blocks or sheets of ice at depth." The results from the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chandrayaan" title="Chandrayaan">
         Chandrayaan
        </a>
       </i>
       mission are also "offering a wide array of watery signals."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On November 13, 2009 NASA announced that the
       <a href="/wiki/LCROSS" title="LCROSS">
        LCROSS
       </a>
       mission had discovered large quantities of water ice on the Moon around the LCROSS impact site at
       <a href="/wiki/Cabeus_(crater)" title="Cabeus (crater)">
        Cabeus
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Zubrin" title="Robert Zubrin">
        Robert Zubrin
       </a>
       , president of the
       <a href="/wiki/Mars_Society" title="Mars Society">
        Mars Society
       </a>
       , relativized the term 'large': "The 30 m crater ejected by the probe contained 10 million kilograms of regolith. Within this ejecta, an estimated 100 kg of water was detected. That represents a proportion of ten parts per million, which is a lower water concentration than that found in the soil of the driest deserts of the Earth. In contrast, we have found continent sized regions on Mars, which are 600,000 parts per million, or 60% water by weight."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although the Moon is very dry on the whole, the spot where the LCROSS impactor hit was chosen for a high concentration of water ice. Dr. Zubrin's computations are not a sound basis for estimating the percentage of water in the regolith at that site. Researchers with expertise in that area estimated that the regolith at the impact site contained 5.6 ± 2.9% water ice, and also noted the presence of other volatile substances. Hydrocarbons, material containing sulfur, carbon dioxide, carbon monoxide, methane and ammonia were present.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psrd.hawaii_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-psrd.hawaii-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In March 2010, NASA reported that the findings of its mini-SAR radar aboard Chandrayaan-1 were consistent with ice deposits at the Moon's north pole. It is estimated there is at least 600 million tons of ice at the north pole in sheets of relatively pure ice at least a couple of meters thick.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In March 2014, researchers who had previously published reports on possible abundance of water on the Moon, reported new findings that refined their predictions substantially lower.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Advantages_and_disadvantages">
        Advantages and disadvantages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Advantages and disadvantages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       For more details on this topic, see
       <a href="/wiki/Space_colonization" title="Space colonization">
        space colonization
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <p>
       Placing a colony on a natural body would provide an ample source of material for construction and other uses in space, including shielding from
       <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">
        cosmic radiation
       </a>
       . The energy required to send objects from the Moon to space is much less than from Earth to space. This could allow the Moon to serve as a source of construction materials within cis-lunar space. Rockets launched from the Moon would require less locally produced propellant than rockets launched from Earth. Some proposals include using electric acceleration devices (
       <a href="/wiki/Mass_driver" title="Mass driver">
        mass drivers
       </a>
       ) to propel objects off the Moon without building rockets. Others have proposed momentum exchange tethers (see below). Furthermore, the Moon does have some
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">
        gravity
       </a>
       , which experience to date indicates may be vital for fetal development and long-term human
       <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">
        health
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Whether the Moon's gravity (roughly one sixth of Earth's) is adequate for this purpose, however, is uncertain.
      </p>
      <p>
       In addition, the Moon is the closest large body in the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       to Earth. While some
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth-crosser_asteroid" title="Earth-crosser asteroid">
        Earth-crosser asteroids
       </a>
       occasionally pass closer, the Moon's distance is consistently within a small range close to 384,400 km. This proximity has several advantages:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        A lunar base could be a site for launching rockets with locally manufactured fuel to distant planets such as Mars. Launching rockets from the Moon would be easier than from Earth because the Moon's gravity is lower, requiring a lower
        <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">
         escape velocity
        </a>
        . A lower escape velocity would require less propellant, but there is no guarantee that less propellant would cost less money than that required to launch from Earth.
        <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_mining" title="Asteroid mining">
         Asteroid mining
        </a>
        , however, may prove useful in lowering various costs accrued during the construction and management of a lunar base and its activities.
       </li>
       <li>
        The energy required to send objects from Earth to the Moon is lower than for most other bodies.
       </li>
       <li>
        Transit time is short. The Apollo astronauts made the trip in three days and future technologies could improve on this time.
       </li>
       <li>
        The short transit time would also allow emergency supplies to quickly reach a Moon colony from Earth, or allow a human crew to evacuate relatively quickly from the Moon to Earth in case of emergency. This could be an important consideration when establishing the first human colony.
       </li>
       <li>
        If the Moon were colonized then it could be tested if humans can survive in low gravity. Those results could be utilized for a
        <a href="/wiki/Colonization_of_Mars" title="Colonization of Mars">
         viable Mars colony
        </a>
        as well.
       </li>
       <li>
        The round trip communication delay to Earth is less than three seconds, allowing near-normal voice and video conversation, and allowing some kinds of remote control of machines from Earth that are not possible for any other celestial body. The delay for other Solar System bodies is minutes or hours; for example, round trip communication time between Earth and Mars ranges from about eight to forty minutes. This, again, could be particularly valuable in an early colony, where life-threatening problems requiring Earth's assistance could occur.
       </li>
       <li>
        On the Lunar near side, the Earth appears large and is always visible as an object 60 times brighter than the Moon appears from Earth, unlike more distant locations where the Earth would be seen merely as a star-like object, much as the planets appear from Earth. As a result, a Lunar colony might feel less remote to humans living there.
       </li>
       <li>
        Building observatory facilities on the Moon from lunar materials allows many of the benefits of space based facilities without the need to launch these into space.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-takahashi-mission-design_47-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-takahashi-mission-design-47">
          [47]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Lunar_soil" title="Lunar soil">
         lunar soil
        </a>
        , although it poses a problem for any moving parts of
        <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">
         telescopes
        </a>
        , can be mixed with
        <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">
         carbon nanotubes
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Epoxy" title="Epoxy">
         epoxies
        </a>
        in the construction of mirrors up to 50 meters in diameter.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
         <a href="#cite_note-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
         <a href="#cite_note-49">
          [49]
         </a>
        </sup>
        It is relatively nearby;
        <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_seeing" title="Astronomical seeing">
         astronomical seeing
        </a>
        is not a concern; certain craters near the poles are permanently dark and cold, and thus especially useful for
        <a href="/wiki/Infrared_telescope" title="Infrared telescope">
         infrared telescopes
        </a>
        ; and
        <a href="/wiki/Radio_telescope" title="Radio telescope">
         radio telescopes
        </a>
        on the far side would be shielded from the radio chatter of Earth.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
         <a href="#cite_note-50">
          [50]
         </a>
        </sup>
        A lunar
        <a href="/wiki/Zenith_telescope" title="Zenith telescope">
         zenith telescope
        </a>
        can be made cheaply with
        <a href="/wiki/Ionic_liquid" title="Ionic liquid">
         ionic liquid
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
         <a href="#cite_note-51">
          [51]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        A farm at the Lunar North Pole could provide eight hours of sunlight per day during the local summer by rotating crops in and out of the sunlight which is continuous for the entire summer. A beneficial temperature, radiation protection, insects for pollination, and all other plant needs could be artificially provided during the local summer for a cost. One estimate suggested a 0.5
        <a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">
         hectare
        </a>
        space farm could feed 100 people.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
         <a href="#cite_note-52">
          [52]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       There are several disadvantages to the Moon as a colony site:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The long lunar night would impede reliance on solar power and require that a colony exposed to the sunlit equatorial surface be designed to withstand large temperature extremes (about 95 K (−178.2 °C) to about 400 K (127 °C)). An exception to this restriction are the so-called "
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peak_of_Eternal_Light" title="Peak of Eternal Light">
         peaks of eternal light
        </a>
        " located at the Lunar north pole that are constantly bathed in sunlight. The rim of
        <a href="/wiki/Shackleton_(crater)" title="Shackleton (crater)">
         Shackleton Crater
        </a>
        , towards the Lunar south pole, also has a near-constant solar illumination. Other areas near the poles that get light most of the time could be linked in a power grid. The temperature 1 meter below the surface of the Moon is estimated to be near constant over the period of a month varying with latitude from near 220 K (−53 °C) at the equator to near 150 K (−123 °C) at the poles.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
         <a href="#cite_note-53">
          [53]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The Moon is highly depleted in
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volatile_elements" title="Volatile elements">
         volatile elements
        </a>
        , such as nitrogen and hydrogen. Carbon, which forms volatile oxides, is also depleted. A number of robot probes including
        <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Prospector" title="Lunar Prospector">
         Lunar Prospector
        </a>
        gathered evidence of hydrogen generally in the Moon's crust consistent with what would be expected from solar wind, and higher concentrations near the poles.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
         <a href="#cite_note-54">
          [54]
         </a>
        </sup>
        There had been some disagreement whether the hydrogen must necessarily be in the form of water. The mission of the
        <a href="/wiki/LCROSS" title="LCROSS">
         Lunar Crater Observation and Sensing Satellite
        </a>
        (LCROSS) proved in 2009 that there is water on the Moon.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
         <a href="#cite_note-55">
          [55]
         </a>
        </sup>
        This water exists in ice form perhaps mixed in small crystals in the
        <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">
         regolith
        </a>
        in a colder landscape than people have ever mined. Other volatiles containing carbon and nitrogen were found in the same cold trap as ice.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psrd.hawaii_42-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-psrd.hawaii-42">
          [42]
         </a>
        </sup>
        If no sufficient means is found for recovering these volatiles on the Moon, they would need to be imported from some other source to support life and industrial processes. Volatiles would need to be stringently recycled. This would limit the colony's rate of growth and keep it dependent on imports. The transportation cost of importing volatiles from Earth could be reduced by constructing the upper stage of supply ships using materials high in volatiles, such as
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graphite-reinforced_plastic" title="Graphite-reinforced plastic">
         carbon fiber
        </a>
        and plastics.
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
        The 2006 announcement by the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keck_Observatory" title="Keck Observatory">
         Keck Observatory
        </a>
        that the binary
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_asteroid" title="Trojan asteroid">
         Trojan asteroid
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/617_Patroclus" title="617 Patroclus">
         617 Patroclus
        </a>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
         <a href="#cite_note-56">
          [56]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and possibly large numbers of other Trojan objects in
        <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
         Jupiter
        </a>
        's orbit, are likely composed of water ice, with a layer of dust, and the hypothesized large amounts of water ice on the closer, main-belt asteroid
        <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">
         1 Ceres
        </a>
        , suggest that importing volatiles from this region via the
        <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_Transport_Network" title="Interplanetary Transport Network">
         Interplanetary Transport Network
        </a>
        may be practical in the not-so-distant future. However, these possibilities are dependent on complicated and expensive resource utilization from the mid to outer Solar System, which is not likely to become available to a Moon colony for a significant period of time.
       </li>
       <li>
        It is uncertain whether the low (one-sixth
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_gravity" title="Earth's gravity">
         <i>
          g
         </i>
        </a>
        ) gravity on the Moon is strong enough to prevent detrimental effects to human health in the long term. Exposure to
        <a href="/wiki/Weightlessness" title="Weightlessness">
         weightlessness
        </a>
        over month-long periods has been demonstrated to cause deterioration of physiological systems, such as loss of bone and muscle mass and a depressed immune system. Similar effects could occur in a low-gravity environment, although virtually all research into the health effects of low gravity has been limited to zero gravity.
       </li>
       <li>
        The lack of a substantial
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_body_atmosphere" title="Celestial body atmosphere">
         atmosphere
        </a>
        for insulation results in temperature extremes and makes the Moon's surface conditions somewhat like a deep space
        <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">
         vacuum
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
        It also leaves the Lunar surface exposed to half as much radiation as in interplanetary space (with the other half blocked by the Moon itself underneath the colony), raising the issues of the
        <a href="/wiki/Health_threat_from_cosmic_rays" title="Health threat from cosmic rays">
         health threat from cosmic rays
        </a>
        and the risk of
        <a href="/wiki/Proton#Human_exposure" title="Proton">
         proton exposure
        </a>
        from the
        <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
         solar wind
        </a>
        . Lunar rubble can protect living quarters from cosmic rays.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
         <a href="#cite_note-57">
          [57]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Shielding against
        <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">
         solar flares
        </a>
        during expeditions outside is more problematic.
       </li>
       <li>
        When the Moon passes through the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetotail" title="Magnetotail">
         magnetotail
        </a>
        of the Earth, the
        <a href="/wiki/Plasma_sheet" title="Plasma sheet">
         plasma sheet
        </a>
        whips across its surface. Electrons crash into the Moon and are released again by UV photons on the day side but build up voltages on the dark side.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
         <a href="#cite_note-58">
          [58]
         </a>
        </sup>
        This causes a negative charge build up from −200 V to −1000 V. See
        <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field_of_the_Moon" title="Magnetic field of the Moon">
         Magnetic field of the Moon
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The lack of an atmosphere increases the chances of the colony being hit by meteors. Even small pebbles and dust (micrometeoroids) have the potential to damage or destroy insufficiently protected structures.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moon_dust" title="Moon dust">
         Moon dust
        </a>
        is an extremely abrasive glassy substance formed by micrometeorites and unrounded due to the lack of weathering. It sticks to everything, can damage equipment, and it may be toxic.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
         <a href="#cite_note-59">
          [59]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Growing crops on the Moon faces many difficult challenges due to the long lunar night (354 hours), extreme variation in surface temperature, exposure to solar flares, nitrogen-poor soil, and lack of insects for pollination. Due to the lack of any atmosphere on the Moon, plants would need to be grown in sealed chambers, though experiments have shown that plants can thrive at pressures much lower than those on Earth.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
         <a href="#cite_note-60">
          [60]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The use of electric lighting to compensate for the 354-hour night might be difficult: a single acre of plants on Earth enjoys a peak 4 megawatts of sunlight power at noon. Experiments conducted by the
        <a href="/wiki/Soviet_space_program" title="Soviet space program">
         Soviet space program
        </a>
        in the 1970s suggest it is possible to grow conventional crops with the 354-hour light, 354-hour dark cycle.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
         <a href="#cite_note-61">
          [61]
         </a>
        </sup>
        A variety of concepts for lunar agriculture have been proposed,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
         <a href="#cite_note-62">
          [62]
         </a>
        </sup>
        including the use of minimal artificial light to maintain plants during the night and the use of fast growing crops that might be started as seedlings with artificial light and be harvestable at the end of one Lunar day.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
         <a href="#cite_note-63">
          [63]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        One of the less obvious difficulties lies not with the Moon itself but rather with the political and national interests of the nations engaged in colonization. Assuming that colonization efforts were able to overcome the difficulties outlined above – there would likely be issues regarding the rights of nations and their colonies to exploit resources on the lunar surface, to stake territorial claims and other issues of sovereignty which would have to be agreed upon before one or more nations established a permanent presence on the Moon. The ongoing negotiations and debate regarding the
        <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">
         Antarctic
        </a>
        is a good case study for prospective lunar colonization efforts in that it highlights the numerous pitfalls of developing/inhabiting a location that is subject to the claims of multiple sovereign nations.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Locations">
        Locations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Locations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       For more details on this topic, see
       <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_the_Moon" title="Geology of the Moon">
        Geology of the Moon
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <p>
       Russian
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">
        astronomer
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vladislav_Vladimirovich_Shevchenko" title="Vladislav Vladimirovich Shevchenko">
        Vladislav V. Shevchenko
       </a>
       proposed in 1988 the following three criteria that a Lunar outpost should meet:
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        good conditions for
        <a href="/wiki/Transport" title="Transport">
         transport
        </a>
        operations;
       </li>
       <li>
        a great number of different types of natural objects and features on the Moon of scientific interest; and
       </li>
       <li>
        natural resources, such as
        <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">
         oxygen
        </a>
        . The abundance of certain minerals, such as
        <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">
         iron oxide
        </a>
        , varies dramatically over the Lunar surface.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
         <a href="#cite_note-64">
          [64]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       While a colony might be located anywhere, potential locations for a Lunar colony fall into three broad categories.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Polar_regions">
        Polar regions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Polar regions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       There are two reasons why the
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_north_pole" title="Lunar north pole">
        north pole
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_south_pole" title="Lunar south pole">
        south pole
       </a>
       of the Moon might be attractive locations for a human colony. First, there is evidence for the presence of water in some continuously shaded areas near the poles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Second, the Moon's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axis_of_rotation" title="Axis of rotation">
        axis of rotation
       </a>
       is sufficiently close to being perpendicular to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plane_of_the_ecliptic" title="Plane of the ecliptic">
        ecliptic plane
       </a>
       that the radius of the Moon's
       <a href="/wiki/Polar_circle" title="Polar circle">
        polar circles
       </a>
       is less than 50 km. Power collection stations could therefore be plausibly located so that at least one is exposed to sunlight at all times, thus making it possible to power polar colonies almost exclusively with solar energy. Solar power would be unavailable only during a
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">
        lunar eclipse
       </a>
       , but these events are relatively brief and absolutely predictable. Any such colony would therefore require a reserve energy supply that could temporarily sustain a colony during lunar eclipses or in the event of any incident or malfunction affecting solar power collection. Hydrogen
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_cells" title="Fuel cells">
        fuel cells
       </a>
       would be ideal for this purpose, since the hydrogen needed could be sourced locally using the Moon's polar water and surplus solar power. Moreover, due to the Moon's uneven surface some sites have nearly continuous sunlight. For example,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malapert_Mountain" title="Malapert Mountain">
        Malapert mountain
       </a>
       , located near the
       <a href="/wiki/Shackleton_(crater)" title="Shackleton (crater)">
        Shackleton crater
       </a>
       at the Lunar south pole, offers several advantages as a site:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        It is exposed to the Sun most of the time (see
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peak_of_Eternal_Light" title="Peak of Eternal Light">
         Peak of Eternal Light
        </a>
        ); two closely spaced arrays of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_module" title="Photovoltaic module">
         solar panels
        </a>
        would receive nearly continuous power.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space_66-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-space-66">
          [66]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Its proximity to Shackleton Crater (116 km, or 69.8 mi) means that it could provide power and communications to the crater. This crater is potentially valuable for
        <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">
         astronomical
        </a>
        observation. An
        <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">
         infrared
        </a>
        instrument would benefit from the very low temperatures. A
        <a href="/wiki/Radio_telescope" title="Radio telescope">
         radio telescope
        </a>
        would benefit from being shielded from Earth's broad spectrum radio interference.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space_66-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-space-66">
          [66]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The nearby
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shoemaker_(Lunar_crater)" title="Shoemaker (Lunar crater)">
         Shoemaker
        </a>
        and other craters are in constant deep shadow, and might contain valuable concentrations of
        <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
         hydrogen
        </a>
        and other volatiles.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space_66-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-space-66">
          [66]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        At around 5,000 meters (16,000 feet) elevation, it offers
        <a href="/wiki/Line-of-sight_propagation" title="Line-of-sight propagation">
         line of sight
        </a>
        communications over a large area of the Moon, as well as to
        <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
         Earth
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space_66-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-space-66">
          [66]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Pole-Aitken_basin" title="South Pole-Aitken basin">
         South Pole-Aitken basin
        </a>
        is located at the Lunar south pole. This is the second largest known impact basin in the Solar System, as well as the oldest and biggest impact feature on the Moon,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esas_67-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-esas-67">
          [67]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and should provide geologists access to deeper layers of the Moon's crust.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       NASA chose to use a south-polar site for the Lunar outpost reference design in the
       <a href="/wiki/Exploration_Systems_Architecture_Study" title="Exploration Systems Architecture Study">
        Exploration Systems Architecture Study
       </a>
       chapter on Lunar Architecture.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esas_67-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-esas-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       At the north pole, the rim of
       <a href="/wiki/Peary_(crater)" title="Peary (crater)">
        Peary Crater
       </a>
       has been proposed as a favorable location for a base.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronomy_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-astronomy-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Examination of images from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clementine_mission" title="Clementine mission">
        Clementine mission
       </a>
       appear to show that parts of the crater rim are permanently illuminated by sunlight (except during
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">
        Lunar eclipses
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronomy_68-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-astronomy-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As a result, the temperature conditions are expected to remain very stable at this location, averaging −50 °C (−58 °F).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronomy_68-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-astronomy-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is comparable to winter conditions in Earth's
       <a href="/wiki/Pole_of_Cold" title="Pole of Cold">
        Poles of Cold
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">
        Siberia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">
        Antarctica
       </a>
       . The interior of Peary Crater may also harbor hydrogen deposits.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronomy_68-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-astronomy-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A 1994
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       bistatic radar experiment performed during the Clementine mission suggested the presence of water ice around the south pole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nozette1996_22-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nozette1996-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.cmf.nrl.navy.mil.622_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-www.cmf.nrl.navy.mil.622-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Prospector" title="Lunar Prospector">
        Lunar Prospector
       </a>
       spacecraft reported enhanced hydrogen abundances at the south pole and even more at the north pole, in 2008.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lunar.arc.nasa.gov.625_71-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-lunar.arc.nasa.gov.625-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On the other hand, results reported using the
       <a href="/wiki/Arecibo_Observatory" title="Arecibo Observatory">
        Arecibo radio telescope
       </a>
       have been interpreted by some to indicate that the anomalous Clementine radar signatures are not indicative of ice, but surface roughness.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.news.cornell.edu.626_72-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-www.news.cornell.edu.626-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This interpretation, however, is not universally agreed upon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A potential limitation of the polar regions is that the inflow of
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
        solar wind
       </a>
       can create an electrical charge on the leeward side of crater rims. The resulting voltage difference can affect electrical equipment, change surface chemistry, erode surfaces and levitate Lunar dust.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Equatorial_regions">
        Equatorial regions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Equatorial regions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Lunar equatorial regions are likely to have higher concentrations of
       <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">
        helium-3
       </a>
       (rare on Earth but much sought after for use in nuclear fusion research) because the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
        solar wind
       </a>
       has a higher
       <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_incidence_(optics)" title="Angle of incidence (optics)">
        angle of incidence
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usra_75-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-usra-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They also enjoy an advantage in extra-Lunar traffic: The rotation advantage for launching material is slight due to the Moon's slow rotation, but the corresponding orbit coincides with the ecliptic, nearly coincides with the Lunar orbit around Earth, and nearly coincides with the equatorial plane of Earth.
      </p>
      <p>
       Several probes have landed in the
       <a href="/wiki/Oceanus_Procellarum" title="Oceanus Procellarum">
        Oceanus Procellarum
       </a>
       area. There are many areas and features that could be subject to long-term study, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Reiner_Gamma" title="Reiner Gamma">
        Reiner Gamma
       </a>
       anomaly and the dark-floored
       <a href="/wiki/Grimaldi_(crater)" title="Grimaldi (crater)">
        Grimaldi crater
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Far_side">
        Far side
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Far side">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Far_side_(Moon)" title="Far side (Moon)">
        Lunar far side
       </a>
       lacks direct communication with Earth, though a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communication_satellite" title="Communication satellite">
        communication satellite
       </a>
       at the
       <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point#L2" title="Lagrangian point">
        L
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">
        Lagrangian point
       </a>
       , or a network of orbiting satellites, could enable communication between the far side of the Moon and Earth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The far side is also a good location for a large radio telescope because it is well shielded from the Earth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Due to the lack of atmosphere, the location is also suitable for an array of
       <a href="/wiki/Optical_telescope" title="Optical telescope">
        optical telescopes
       </a>
       , similar to the
       <a href="/wiki/Very_Large_Telescope" title="Very Large Telescope">
        Very Large Telescope
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">
        Chile
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-takahashi-mission-design_47-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-takahashi-mission-design-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
