       To date, there has been no ground exploration of the far side.
      </p>
      <p>
       Scientists have estimated that the highest concentrations of helium-3 will be found in the
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_mare" title="Lunar mare">
        maria
       </a>
       on the far side, as well as near side areas containing concentrations of the
       <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">
        titanium
       </a>
       -based
       <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">
        mineral
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Ilmenite" title="Ilmenite">
        ilmenite
       </a>
       . On the near side the Earth and its magnetic field partially shields the surface from the solar wind during each orbit. But the far side is fully exposed, and thus should receive a somewhat greater proportion of the ion stream.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_lava_tubes">
        Lunar lava tubes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Lunar lava tubes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mare_Tranquillitatis_pit_crater.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="800" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Mare_Tranquillitatis_pit_crater.jpg/220px-Mare_Tranquillitatis_pit_crater.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Mare_Tranquillitatis_pit_crater.jpg/330px-Mare_Tranquillitatis_pit_crater.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Mare_Tranquillitatis_pit_crater.jpg/440px-Mare_Tranquillitatis_pit_crater.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mare_Tranquillitatis_pit_crater.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         High Sun view of a 100 meter deep Lunar pit crater that may provide access to a lava tube
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_lava_tube" title="Lunar lava tube">
        Lunar lava tubes
       </a>
       are a potential location for constructing a Lunar base. Any intact lava tube on the Moon could serve as a shelter from the severe environment of the Lunar surface, with its frequent meteorite impacts, high-energy ultra-violet radiation and energetic particles, and extreme diurnal temperature variations. Lava tubes provide ideal positions for shelter because of their access to nearby resources. They also have proven themselves as a reliable structure, having withstood the test of time for billions of years.
      </p>
      <p>
       An underground colony would escape the extreme of temperature on the Moon's surface. The average temperature on the surface of the Moon is about −5 °C. The day period (about 354 hours) has an average temperature of about 107 °C (225 °F), although it can rise as high as 123 °C (253 °F). The night period (also 354 hours) has an average temperature of about −153 °C (−243 °F).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Underground, both periods would be around −23 °C (−9 °F), and humans could install ordinary heaters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       One such lava tube was discovered in early 2009.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">
        Structure
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Structure">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat">
        Habitat
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Habitat">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       There have been numerous proposals regarding habitat modules. The designs have evolved throughout the years as mankind's knowledge about the Moon has grown, and as the technological possibilities have changed. The proposed habitats range from the actual spacecraft landers or their used fuel tanks, to inflatable modules of various shapes. Some hazards of the Lunar environment such as sharp temperature shifts, lack of atmosphere or magnetic field (which means higher levels of radiation and micrometeoroids) and long nights, were unknown early on. Proposals have shifted as these hazards were recognized and taken into consideration.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Underground_colonies">
        Underground colonies
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Underground colonies">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Some suggest building the Lunar colony underground, which would give protection from radiation and micrometeoroids. This would also greatly reduce the risk of air leakage, as the colony would be fully sealed from the outside except for a few exits to the surface.
      </p>
      <p>
       The construction of an underground base would probably be more complex; one of the first machines from Earth might be a remote-controlled excavating machine. Once created, some sort of hardening would be necessary to avoid collapse, possibly a
       <a href="/wiki/Shotcrete" title="Shotcrete">
        spray-on
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">
        concrete
       </a>
       -like substance made from available materials.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iht_82-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-iht-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A more porous insulating material also made
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In-situ_resource_utilization" title="In-situ resource utilization">
        <i>
         in-situ
        </i>
       </a>
       could then be applied. Rowley &amp; Neudecker have suggested "melt-as-you-go" machines that would leave glassy internal surfaces.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">
        Mining
       </a>
       methods such as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Room_and_pillar" title="Room and pillar">
        room and pillar
       </a>
       might also be used. Inflatable self-sealing fabric habitats might then be put in place to retain air. Eventually an
       <a href="/wiki/Underground_city" title="Underground city">
        underground city
       </a>
       can be constructed. Farms set up underground would need
       <a href="/wiki/Artificial_sunlight" title="Artificial sunlight">
        artificial sunlight
       </a>
       . As an alternative to excavating, a
       <a href="/wiki/Lava_tube" title="Lava tube">
        lava tube
       </a>
       could be covered and insulated, thus solving the problem of radiation exposure.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_colonies">
        Surface colonies
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Surface colonies">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:01LunarHouse.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="644" data-file-width="1137" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/01LunarHouse.png/220px-01LunarHouse.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/01LunarHouse.png/330px-01LunarHouse.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/01LunarHouse.png/440px-01LunarHouse.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:01LunarHouse.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Variant for habitat creation on the surface or over lava tube
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inflated_lunar_habitat_outside.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Inflated_lunar_habitat_outside.jpg/220px-Inflated_lunar_habitat_outside.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Inflated_lunar_habitat_outside.jpg/330px-Inflated_lunar_habitat_outside.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Inflated_lunar_habitat_outside.jpg/440px-Inflated_lunar_habitat_outside.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inflated_lunar_habitat_outside.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A NASA model of a proposed
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inflatable_module" title="Inflatable module">
          inflatable module
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A possibly easier solution would be to build the Lunar base on the surface, and cover the modules with Lunar soil. The Lunar
       <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">
        regolith
       </a>
       is composed of a unique blend of silica and iron-containing compounds that may be fused into a glass-like solid using microwave energy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Blacic has studied the mechanical properties of lunar glass and has shown that it is a promising material for making rigid structures, if coated with metal to keep moisture out.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This may allow for the use of "Lunar bricks" in structural designs, or the vitrification of loose dirt to form a hard, ceramic crust.
      </p>
      <p>
       A Lunar base built on the surface would need to be protected by improved radiation and micrometeoroid shielding. Building the Lunar base inside a deep crater would provide at least partial shielding against radiation and micrometeoroids. Artificial magnetic fields have been proposed
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as a means to provide radiation shielding for long range deep space manned missions, and it might be possible to use similar technology on a Lunar colony. Some regions on the Moon possess strong local magnetic fields that might partially mitigate exposure to charged solar and galactic particles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In a turn from the usual engineer-designed lunar habitats,
       <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">
        London
       </a>
       -based
       <a href="/wiki/Foster_and_Partners" title="Foster and Partners">
        Foster + Partners
       </a>
       architectural firm proposed a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3D_printer" title="3D printer">
        building construction 3D-printer
       </a>
       technology in January 2013 that would use Lunar regolith raw materials to produce Lunar building structures while using
       <a href="/wiki/Inflatable_space_habitat" title="Inflatable space habitat">
        enclosed inflatable habitats
       </a>
       for housing the human occupants inside the hard-shell Lunar structures. Overall, these habitats would require only ten percent of the structure mass to be
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_transport" title="Space transport">
        transported
       </a>
       from Earth, while using local Lunar materials for the other 90 percent of the structure mass.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gizmodo20130131_89-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-gizmodo20130131-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       "Printed" Lunar soil will provide both "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_radiation" title="Space radiation">
        radiation
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_thermal_control" title="Spacecraft thermal control">
        temperature
       </a>
       insulation. Inside, a lightweight pressurized inflatable with the same dome shape will be the living environment for the first human Moon settlers."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gizmodo20130131_89-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-gizmodo20130131-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The building technology will include mixing Lunar material with
       <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_oxide" title="Magnesium oxide">
        magnesium oxide
       </a>
       , which will turn the "moonstuff into a pulp that can be sprayed to form the block" when a binding salt is applied that "converts [this] material into a stone-like solid."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gizmodo20130131_89-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-gizmodo20130131-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Terrestrial versions of this 3D-printing building technology are already printing 2 metres (6 ft 7 in) of building material per hour with the next-generation printers capable of 3.5 metres (11 ft) per hour, sufficient to complete a building in a week.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gizmodo20130131_89-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-gizmodo20130131-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Moon_Capital">
        Moon Capital
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Moon Capital">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 2010, The Moon Capital Competition offered a prize for a design of a Lunar habitat intended to be an underground international commercial center capable of supporting a residential staff of 60 people and their families. The Moon Capital is intended to be self-sufficient with respect to food and other material required for life support. Prize money was provided primarily by the
       <a href="/wiki/Boston_Society_of_Architects" title="Boston Society of Architects">
        Boston Society of Architects
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Google_Lunar_X_Prize" title="Google Lunar X Prize">
        Google Lunar X Prize
       </a>
       and The New England Council of the
       <a href="/wiki/American_Institute_of_Aeronautics_and_Astronautics" title="American Institute of Aeronautics and Astronautics">
        American Institute of Aeronautics and Astronautics
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="3D_printed_structures">
        3D printed structures
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: 3D printed structures">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       On January 31, 2013, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESA" title="ESA">
        ESA
       </a>
       working with an independent architectural firm, tested a
       <a href="/wiki/3D_printing" title="3D printing">
        3D-printed
       </a>
       structure that could be constructed of lunar
       <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">
        regolith
       </a>
       for use as a Moon base.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
        <a href="#cite_note-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">
        Energy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Energy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_power">
        Nuclear power
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Nuclear power">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       A nuclear
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_reactor" title="Fission reactor">
        fission reactor
       </a>
       might fulfill most of a Moon base's power requirements.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schierholz_92-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schierholz-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       With the help of fission reactors, one could overcome the difficulty of the 354 hour Lunar night. According to NASA, a nuclear fission power station could generate a steady 40 kilowatts, equivalent to the demand of about eight houses on Earth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schierholz_92-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schierholz-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An artist’s concept of such a station published by NASA envisages the reactor being buried below the Moon's surface to shield it from its surroundings; out from a tower-like generator part reaching above the surface over the reactor, radiators would extend into space to send away any heat energy that may be left over.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">
        Radioisotope thermoelectric generators
       </a>
       could be used as backup and emergency power sources for solar powered colonies.
      </p>
      <p>
       One specific development program in the 2000s was the Fission Surface Power (FSP) project of
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_DOE" title="US DOE">
        DOE
       </a>
       , a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_power_system" title="Fission power system">
        fission power system
       </a>
       focused on "developing and demonstrating a nominal 40 kWe power system to support human exploration missions. The FSP system concept uses conventional
       <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel#Types_of_stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">
        low-temperature stainless steel
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_metal-cooled_reactor" title="Liquid metal-cooled reactor">
        liquid metal-cooled reactor
       </a>
       technology coupled with
       <a href="/wiki/Stirling_cycle" title="Stirling cycle">
        Stirling power
       </a>
       conversion." As of 2010
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , significant component hardware testing had been successfully completed, and a non-nuclear system demonstration test was being fabricated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa/doe2010_94-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa/doe2010-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Precise_language" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers">
          <span title="The text near this tag is dated.">
           needs update
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">
        Helium-3
       </a>
       mining could be used to provide a substitute for
       <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">
        tritium
       </a>
       for potential production of
       <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">
        fusion power
       </a>
       in the future.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_energy">
        Solar energy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Solar energy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       For more details on this topic, see
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peak_of_Eternal_Light" title="Peak of Eternal Light">
        Peak of Eternal Light
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <p>
       Solar energy is a possible source of power for a Lunar base. Many of the raw materials needed for solar panel production can be extracted on site. However, the long Lunar night (354 hours) is a drawback for solar power on the Moon's surface. This might be solved by building several power plants, so that at least one of them is always in daylight. Another possibility would be to build such a power plant where there is constant or near-constant sunlight, such as at the Malapert mountain near the Lunar south pole, or on the rim of
       <a href="/wiki/Peary_(crater)" title="Peary (crater)">
        Peary crater
       </a>
       near the north pole. Since lunar regolith contains structural metals like iron and aluminum, solar panels could be mounted high up on locally-built towers that might rotate to follow the sun. A third possibility would be to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_solar_power" title="Space solar power">
        leave the panels in orbit
       </a>
       , and beam the power down as microwaves.
      </p>
      <p>
       The solar energy converters need not be
       <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">
        silicon
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_module" title="Photovoltaic module">
        solar panels
       </a>
       . It may be more advantageous to use the larger temperature difference between Sun and shade to run
       <a href="/wiki/Heat_engine" title="Heat engine">
        heat engine
       </a>
       generators. Concentrated sunlight could also be relayed via mirrors and used in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stirling_engines" title="Stirling engines">
        Stirling engines
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_trough" title="Solar trough">
        solar trough
       </a>
       generators, or it could be used directly for lighting, agriculture and process heat. The focused heat might also be employed in materials processing to extract various elements from Lunar surface materials.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_storage">
        Energy storage
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Energy storage">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Fuel cells on the
       <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">
        Space Shuttle
       </a>
       have operated reliably for up to 17 Earth days at a time. On the Moon, they would only be needed for 354 hours (14 ​
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        <sup>
         3
        </sup>
        ⁄
        <sub>
         4
        </sub>
       </span>
       days) – the length of the Lunar night. Fuel cells produce water directly as a waste product. Current fuel cell technology is more advanced than the Shuttle's cells –
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_exchange_membrane_fuel_cell" title="Proton exchange membrane fuel cell">
        PEM (Proton Exchange Membrane) cells
       </a>
       produce considerably less heat (though their waste heat would likely be useful during the Lunar night) and are lighter, not to mention the reduced mass of the smaller heat-dissipating radiators. This makes PEMs more economical to launch from Earth than the shuttle's cells. PEMs have not yet been proven in space.
      </p>
      <p>
       Combining fuel cells with electrolysis would provide a "perpetual" source of electricity – solar energy could be used to provide power during the Lunar day, and fuel cells at night. During the Lunar day, solar energy would also be used to electrolyze the water created in the fuel cells – although there would be small losses of gases that would have to be replaced.
      </p>
      <p>
       Even if lunar colonies could provide themselves access to a near-continuous source of solar energy, they would still need to maintain fuel cells or an alternate energy storage system to sustain themselves during lunar eclipses and emergency situations.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">
        Transport
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Transport">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_to_Moon">
        Earth to Moon
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Earth to Moon">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Conventional
       <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">
        rockets
       </a>
       have been used for most Lunar explorations to date. The ESA's
       <a href="/wiki/SMART-1" title="SMART-1">
        SMART-1
       </a>
       mission from 2003 to 2006 used conventional chemical rockets to reach orbit and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hall_effect_thruster" title="Hall effect thruster">
        Hall effect thrusters
       </a>
       to arrive at the Moon in 13 months. NASA would have used chemical rockets on its
       <a href="/wiki/Ares_V" title="Ares V">
        Ares V
       </a>
       booster and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Surface_Access_Module" title="Lunar Surface Access Module">
        Lunar Surface Access Module
       </a>
       , that were being developed for a planned return to the Moon around 2019, but this was cancelled. The construction workers, location finders, and other astronauts vital to building, would have been taken four at a time in NASA's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orion_(Constellation_program)" title="Orion (Constellation program)">
        Orion
       </a>
       spacecraft.
      </p>
      <p>
       Proposed concepts of Earth-Moon transportation are
       <a href="/wiki/Space_elevator" title="Space elevator">
        Space elevators
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="On_the_surface">
        On the surface
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: On the surface">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_rover_concept_drawing_s94_27631.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="530" data-file-width="640" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Lunar_rover_concept_drawing_s94_27631.jpg/220px-Lunar_rover_concept_drawing_s94_27631.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Lunar_rover_concept_drawing_s94_27631.jpg/330px-Lunar_rover_concept_drawing_s94_27631.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Lunar_rover_concept_drawing_s94_27631.jpg/440px-Lunar_rover_concept_drawing_s94_27631.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_rover_concept_drawing_s94_27631.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A Lunar rover being unloaded from a cargo spacecraft. Conceptual drawing
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Lunar colonists will want the ability to transport cargo and people to and from modules and spacecraft, and to carry out scientific study of a larger area of the Lunar surface for long periods of time. Proposed concepts include a variety of vehicle designs, from small open rovers to large pressurized modules with lab equipment, and also a few flying or hopping vehicles.
      </p>
      <p>
       Rovers could be useful if the terrain is not too steep or hilly. The only rovers to have operated on the surface of the Moon (as of 2008
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) are the three Apollo
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Roving_Vehicle" title="Lunar Roving Vehicle">
        Lunar Roving Vehicles
       </a>
       (LRV), developed by
       <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">
        Boeing
       </a>
       , the two robotic Soviet
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunokhod" title="Lunokhod">
        Lunokhods
       </a>
       and the Chinese Yutu rover in 2013. The LRV was an open rover for a crew of two, and a range of 92 km during one Lunar day. One
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       study resulted in the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mobile_Lunar_Laboratory&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mobile Lunar Laboratory (page does not exist)">
        Mobile Lunar Laboratory
       </a>
       concept, a manned pressurized rover for a crew of two, with a range of 396 km. The Soviet Union developed different rover concepts in the Lunokhod series and the L5 for possible use on future manned missions to the Moon or Mars. These rover designs were all pressurized for longer sorties.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rsw_97-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rsw-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       If multiple bases were established on the Lunar surface, they could be linked together by permanent railway systems. Both conventional and
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_levitation" title="Magnetic levitation">
        magnetic levitation
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Maglev" title="Maglev">
        Maglev
       </a>
       ) systems have been proposed for the transport lines. Mag-Lev systems are particularly attractive as there is no atmosphere on the surface to slow down the
       <a href="/wiki/Train" title="Train">
        train
       </a>
       , so the vehicles could achieve velocities comparable to
       <a href="/wiki/Aircraft" title="Aircraft">
        aircraft
       </a>
       on the Earth. One significant difference with lunar trains, however, is that the cars would need to be individually sealed and possess their own life support systems.
      </p>
      <p>
       For difficult areas, a flying vehicle may be more suitable.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bell_Aerosystems" title="Bell Aerosystems">
        Bell Aerosystems
       </a>
       proposed their design for the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lunar_Flying_Vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lunar Flying Vehicle (page does not exist)">
        Lunar Flying Vehicle
       </a>
       as part of a study for NASA. Bell also developed the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Manned_Flying_System&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Manned Flying System (page does not exist)">
        Manned Flying System
       </a>
       , a similar concept.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_to_space">
        Surface to space
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Surface to space">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_technology">
        Launch technology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Launch technology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_base_concept_drawing_s78_23252.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="675" data-file-width="1023" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Lunar_base_concept_drawing_s78_23252.jpg/220px-Lunar_base_concept_drawing_s78_23252.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Lunar_base_concept_drawing_s78_23252.jpg/330px-Lunar_base_concept_drawing_s78_23252.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Lunar_base_concept_drawing_s78_23252.jpg/440px-Lunar_base_concept_drawing_s78_23252.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_base_concept_drawing_s78_23252.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A Lunar base with a mass driver (the long structure that goes toward the horizon). NASA conceptual illustration
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Experience so far indicates that launching human beings into space is much more expensive than launching cargo.
      </p>
      <p>
       One way to get materials and products from the Moon to an interplanetary way station might be with a
       <a href="/wiki/Mass_driver" title="Mass driver">
        mass driver
       </a>
       , a magnetically accelerated projectile launcher. Cargo would be picked up from orbit or an Earth-Moon
       <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">
        Lagrangian point
       </a>
       by a shuttle craft using
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_propulsion" title="Ion propulsion">
        ion propulsion
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_sail" title="Solar sail">
        solar sails
       </a>
       or other means and delivered to Earth orbit or other destinations such as near-Earth asteroids,
       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
        Mars
       </a>
       or other planets, perhaps using the
       <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_Transport_Network" title="Interplanetary Transport Network">
        Interplanetary Transport Network
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       A
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_space_elevator" title="Lunar space elevator">
        Lunar space elevator
       </a>
       could transport people, raw materials and products to and from an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_station" title="Orbital station">
        orbital station
       </a>
       at Lagrangian points
       <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point#L1" title="Lagrangian point">
        L
        <sub>
         1
        </sub>
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point#L2" title="Lagrangian point">
        L
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
       </a>
       . Chemical rockets would take a payload from Earth to the
       <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point#L1" title="Lagrangian point">
        L1 Lunar Lagrange
       </a>
       location. From there a tether would slowly lower the payload to a soft landing on the lunar surface.
      </p>
      <p>
       Other possibilities include a
       <a href="/wiki/Momentum_exchange_tether#Cislunar_transportation_system" title="Momentum exchange tether">
        momentum exchange tether system
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_costs">
        Launch costs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Launch costs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Estimates of the cost per unit mass of launching cargo or people from the Moon vary and the cost impacts of future technological improvements are difficult to predict. An upper bound on the cost of launching material from the Moon might be about $40,000,000 per kilogram, based on dividing the
        <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">
         Apollo program
        </a>
        costs by the amount of material returned.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
         <a href="#cite_note-98">
          [98]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
         <a href="#cite_note-99">
          [99]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
         <a href="#cite_note-100">
          [100]
         </a>
        </sup>
        At the other extreme, the incremental cost of launching material from the Moon using an electromagnetic accelerator could be quite low. The efficiency of launching material from the Moon with a proposed electric accelerator is suggested to be about 50%.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
         <a href="#cite_note-101">
          [101]
         </a>
        </sup>
        If the carriage of a mass driver weighs the same as the cargo, two kilograms must be accelerated to orbital velocity for each kilogram put into orbit. The overall system efficiency would then drop to 25%. So 1.4 kilowatt-hours would be needed to launch an incremental kilogram of cargo to low orbit from the Moon.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
         <a href="#cite_note-102">
          [102]
         </a>
        </sup>
        At $0.1/kilowatt-hour, a typical cost for electrical power on Earth, that amounts to $0.16 for the energy to launch a kilogram of cargo into orbit. For the actual cost of an operating system, energy loss for power conditioning, the cost of radiating waste heat, the cost of maintaining all systems, and the interest cost of the capital investment are considerations.
       </li>
       <li>
        Passengers cannot be divided into the parcel size suggested for the cargo of a mass driver, nor subjected to hundreds of gravities acceleration. However, technical developments could also affect the cost of launching passengers to orbit from the Moon. Instead of bringing all fuel and oxidizer from Earth, liquid oxygen could be produced from lunar materials and hydrogen should be available from the lunar poles. The cost of producing these on the Moon is yet unknown, but they will be more expensive than production costs on Earth. The situation of the local hydrogen is most open to speculation. As a rocket fuel, hydrogen could be extended by combining it chemically with silicon to form
        <a href="/wiki/Silane" title="Silane">
         silane
        </a>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
         <a href="#cite_note-103">
          [103]
         </a>
        </sup>
        which has yet to be demonstrated in an actual rocket engine. In the absence of more technical developments, the cost of transporting people from the Moon will be an impediment to colonization.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_to_and_from_cis-Lunar_space">
        Surface to and from cis-Lunar space
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Surface to and from cis-Lunar space">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       A
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cislunar_space" title="Cislunar space">
        cis-Lunar
       </a>
       transport system has been proposed using tethers to achieve momentum exchange.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
        <a href="#cite_note-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This system requires zero net energy input, and could not only retrieve payloads from the Lunar surface and transport them to Earth, but could also soft land payloads on to the Lunar surface.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_development">
        Economic development
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Economic development">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <span id="Lunar_mining">
       </span>
       For long term sustainability, a space colony should be close to self-sufficient.
       <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">
        Mining
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Refining" title="Refining">
        refining
       </a>
       the Moon's materials on-site – for use both on the Moon and elsewhere in the Solar System – could provide an advantage over deliveries from Earth, as they can be launched into space at a much lower energy cost than from Earth. It is possible that large amounts of matter will need to be launched into space for interplanetary
       <a href="/wiki/Exploration" title="Exploration">
        exploration
       </a>
       in the 21st century, and the lower cost of providing goods from the Moon might be attractive.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iht_82-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-iht-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Space-based_materials_processing">
        Space-based materials processing
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Space-based materials processing">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In the long term, the Moon will likely play an important role in supplying space-based construction facilities with raw materials.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rsw_97-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rsw-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Zero gravity in space allows for the processing of materials in ways impossible or difficult on Earth, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Metal_foam" title="Metal foam">
        "foaming" metals
       </a>
       , where a gas is injected into a molten metal, and then the metal is
       <a href="/wiki/Annealing_(metallurgy)" title="Annealing (metallurgy)">
        annealed
       </a>
       slowly. On Earth, the gas bubbles rise and burst, but in a
       <a href="/wiki/Weightlessness" title="Weightlessness">
        zero gravity
       </a>
       environment, that does not happen. The
       <a href="/wiki/Annealing_(metallurgy)" title="Annealing (metallurgy)">
        annealing
       </a>
       process requires large amounts of energy, as a material is kept very hot for an extended period of time. (This allows the molecular structure to realign.)
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Exporting_material_to_Earth">
        Exporting material to Earth
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Exporting material to Earth">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Exporting material to Earth in trade from the Moon is more problematic due to the cost of transportation, which will vary greatly if the Moon is industrially developed (see "Launch costs" above). One suggested trade commodity,
       <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">
        Helium-3
       </a>
       (
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       He) from the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">
        solar wind
       </a>
       , is thought to have accumulated on the Moon's surface over billions of years, but occurs only rarely on Earth. Helium might be present in the Lunar
       <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">
        regolith
       </a>
       in quantities of 0.01 ppm to 0.05 ppm (depending on soil). In 2006
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       It had a market price of about $1500 per gram ($1.5M per kilogram), more than 120 times the value per unit weight of
       <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">
        gold
       </a>
       and over eight times the value of
       <a href="/wiki/Rhodium" title="Rhodium">
        rhodium
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In the future
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       He may have a role as a fuel in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermonuclear" title="Thermonuclear">
        thermonuclear
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fusion_reactor" title="Fusion reactor">
        fusion reactors
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
        <a href="#cite_note-105">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It should require about 100 tonnes of helium-3 to produce the electricity that Earth uses in a year and there should be enough on the moon to provide that much for 10,000 years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
        <a href="#cite_note-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Exporting_propellant_obtained_from_lunar_water">
        Exporting propellant obtained from lunar water
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Exporting propellant obtained from lunar water">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       To reduce the cost of transport, the Moon could store
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISRU" title="ISRU">
        propellants produced from lunar water
       </a>
       at one or several
       <a href="/wiki/Propellant_depot" title="Propellant depot">
        depots
       </a>
       between the Earth and the Moon, to resupply rockets or satellites in Earth orbit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
        <a href="#cite_note-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Shackleton_Energy_Company" title="Shackleton Energy Company">
        Shackleton Energy Company
       </a>
       estimate investment in this infrastructure could cost around $25 billion.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
        <a href="#cite_note-108">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_power_satellites">
        Solar power satellites
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Colonization_of_the_Moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Solar power satellites">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Gerard_K._O%27Neill" title="Gerard K. O'Neill">
        Gerard K. O'Neill
       </a>
       , noting the problem of high launch costs in the early 1970s, came up with the idea of building
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_Power_Satellite" title="Solar Power Satellite">
        Solar Power Satellites
       </a>
       in orbit with materials from the Moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
        <a href="#cite_note-109">
         [109]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Launch costs from the Moon will vary greatly if the Moon is industrially developed (see "Launch costs" above). This proposal was based on the contemporary estimates of future launch costs of the space shuttle.
      </p>
      <p>
       On 30 April 1979 the Final Report "Lunar Resources Utilization for Space Construction" by General Dynamics Convair Division under NASA contract NAS9-15560 concluded that use of Lunar resources would be cheaper than terrestrial materials for a system comprising as few as thirty Solar Power Satellites of 10 GW capacity each.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
        <a href="#cite_note-110">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1980, when it became obvious NASA's launch cost estimates for the space shuttle were grossly optimistic, O'Neill et al. published another route to manufacturing using Lunar materials with much lower startup costs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
        <a href="#cite_note-111">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This 1980s SPS concept relied less on human presence in space and more on partially self-replicating systems on the Lunar surface under
       <a href="/wiki/Telepresence" title="Telepresence">
        telepresence
       </a>
       control of workers stationed on Earth.
      </p>

             ^

          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           <cite class="citation web">
            David Darling.
            <a class="external text" href="http://daviddarling.info/encyclopedia/M/mass_driver.html" rel="nofollow">
             "mass driver"
            </a>
            .

           <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Internet+Encyclopedia+of+Science&amp;rft.atitle=mass+driver&amp;rft.au=David+Darling&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fdaviddarling.info%2Fencyclopedia%2FM%2Fmass_driver.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AColonization+of+the+Moon">
            <span style="display:none;">
            </span>
           </span>
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-102">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-102">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The circular
           <a href="/wiki/Orbital_speed" title="Orbital speed">
            orbital speed
           </a>
           for any central body equals the square root of the quantity (the radius of the orbit times the gravity of the central body at that point); for the Lunar surface: the square root of (1,730,000 meters times 1.63 meters per second squared) is 1680 meters per second. The energy of this motion for one kilogram is one half the square of the speed, 1,410,000 watt seconds or 0.392 kilowatt-hours. With a 25% efficient accelerator, 1.6 kilowatt-hours are needed to achieve the orbital velocity.
          </span>
         </li>
